J^OFIA, Aug. 31. â hi. both official and revolutionary circles
Â» the opinion is freely held that war between Bulgaria antl
Turkey-is imminent and can be averted by nothing short of
a miracle. It is not e.xpected that either Government will form
ally declare hostilities, jbut that the prevailing conditions will force
warfare.
There is no question -that .large numbers of insurgents have
recently- crossed the frontier.: An extensive outbreak in North
ern Macedonia is possible at any time. The Autonome prints a'
telegram f rom . Constantinople declaring that the -Sultan/-in
fluenced by .the counsels of Germany, now. favors war "with Bul
garia. The Turks here, however, take an pptimistic yiew, assert
ing that.there is no danger of a war, as Turkey not "desire
one, and Prince Ferdinand and the present Bulgarian Govern
ment fire not in. a position to force hostilities. " â¢ $;5
The Bulgar-ian agent^at Uskiib reports thaf. detachments of
Turkish troops sent to garrison-, the small towns in that vilayet
have spread destruction-along their route; the' villagers have been
robbed and, beaten, the women violated and the Christian popula
tion subjected to every conceivable outrage, while the local au
thorities appear to be helpless to stop the hostilities. *,
Up to'6 o'clock this evening'Prince Ferdinand had not ar
rived in Sofia 1 and the court^bfficiars. declare that they are without
information as to his movements: . â¢ . ' . ' : :* ';
â¢ . Nine hundred refugees from Malkotirnovo and surrounding
villages have arrived at Ururhku, Bulgaria.. The.fefugees say the
Turkish garrisoji fired upon- the Bulgarjanpart of Malkotirnovo,
with the object of creating an impression that a revolution was in
progress. . â¢Â¦ /- ;"; " Â¦": ....
The .Prefect, August 21/ summoned the Bulgarian notables-,
of Malkotirnovo and endeavored'to force them to sign a statement
that the insurgents were the aggressors. The notables, refused to
do so and the first man who refused" was hanged the next day.
The Macedonian leaders here claim that an aggregate of 2000
Bulgarians have crossed .the;. frontier, in small parties antf'joined
the insurgents' during the last; ten; days. General Zonte Hoff, presi
dent of ihe -Macedonian committee, and Colonel Jankoff, one of
the revolutionary leaders, -who I are . now in -Macedonia,' sent . word
that the! insurgent. forces total" i 2,000 to 1 5,000 men. They:ex
pect that a^general insurrection will be proclaimed this week.

The following letter was received by
Mrs.. White before her marriage:
San Francisco. Cal.; Nov. 16, 1881.
Dear Jennie: I got two letters from you
this afternoon and postofflce order for $100.
You have acted very sensibly by not sending
the money by telegraph. 1 always admittPd
your good saving sense. It will come very
handy just now, as my clothes are very poor
and my present salary is so small that I can
not afford to buy more. However, I will now
buy a cheap suit and look, "cleaner. . . .
Good-bye. Ycurs, . ' .,y LLOYD.

December 18, 1S81.
Dear Jennie: Your letter came to hand .'yes
terday and I was astonished to see by It that

every Sunday since you left and mailed it my
self, so I can't understand why you did not
get it. 1 am so glad your toe Is getting bet

sore on the feet, as one feels It so much In
that place, j However, I trust by the time this
reaches you that you will be cured . entirely.

â¢How I would like to be able to afford to take

China or, in fact, any place; Jennie, at your
expenws or my own. A great many men would
only be too glad to accept such a trip and
spend your money for you. ... I hope
you will have a very 'happy Xmas, as I. can
now assure you I will have a very miserable
one. Jennie, think of me during part of that
day. Good-bye until next letter. Yours.

by the late Jonathan Lloyd "White
to the woman who became his
wife, were read in evidence yes
terday before Judge*Troutt In the
case of Mrs. Jennie White against Ihe ex
ecutors of her deceased husband's estate.
The case is a peculiar one. It was
brought recently by the wife, many,
years after the dtath of White, who
lost bis life- while rescuing som'e^ women
in the memorable Baldwin Hotel fire, to
eet aside a decree of divorce granted the

the grourid of desertion and the wife is
now attempting to prove that she never

his attempt to secure -a divorce. She
learned of his success some ye'ars after
her marriage to him, when she was in
New York, where she was staying at his
urgent request- ... ,".;.'-
The evidence so far introduced proves
that 'Jonathan Lloyd. White led a dual
life. The attorneys for Mrs. White â¢
set up the claim that White prac
ticed fraud on his wife and secured her
money, with which he accumulated a vast
fortune.

come In for her share of the prop
erty. The executors and heirs are
combating Mrs. White and the trial which

W. Aitken are representing* Mrs. Jennie
White and \V. K. Johnson and W. B.

of the heirs and executors.
LOVE LETTERS ARE READ.
Mrs. White is a well preserved woman.
She goes to court in somber black and is
accompanied by her beautiful niece, Miss
May Stafford of New York. Mrs. White
bases her case on lettcts which were
written to her by White before and after
their marriage. There are more than fifty
and according to the witness they are only
a few of the many he wrote her. These
letters are full of endearment and, in the
light of events that have passed, prove
that the writer was a consummate actor.
According to the evidence, which are
written admissions in the handwriting of
the deceased. White never had more than
a thousand dollars before he met the wo
man he married. In his letters he con
tinually asks for the advancement of
money In order that he may make a fpr
tunc for both and live happily and in
comfort. When Mrs. White married she
had nearly $30,000, and it was with this
fortune that White was able to accumu
late the money that he left to his heirs.

Troutt yesterday morning, the plaintiff,

married to Jonathan Lloyd White in 18S3
and that they had been engaged for more

THE fourth annual convention of
the German Roman Catholic
Staats Verband held its second
and last session yesterday. The
meeting was lengthy and was
continued throughout the day. ,
Religious ceremonies marked the open
ing of the session, when a solemn
requiem, mass was performed at St. Boni
face Church for the deceased members
and benefactors of the verhand. The Rev.
Father W. JJelchers of San Jose was cel
ebrant, the Rev. Father P. Josaphat
Krausa assisted as deacon and the Rev.
Father P. George Wehmelr of Sacra
mento- was sub-deacon.
' Later the meeting was convened by the
president, F. B. â¢ Schvenstein, when
George Buecker, vice president of Cali
fornia of the Roman Catholic Central
Verein of America, spoke in favor of the
Central Verein, telling of Its benefits and
asking those few societies not yet affili
ated to become part of the organization.
The matter of members taking out their
naturalization papers was brought before
the meeting by Ludwig von Kresfeld,
delegate from San Jose, who advocated
that the members of the various organi
zations should take an interest In both
local and national politics. '
At the afternoon session the various
committees submitted reports, which
were duly received and adopted. It was
resolved to have the proceedings of the
convention printed in pamphlet form, as
usual.
The duties of Catholic laymen was the
subject of a discourse by S. Peisel, and
L. Kaempfeu delivered an address on
"The Mother Tongue."
The hearty thanks of the delegates
were tendered those who had worked so
diligently toward making the convention
a sqeccss. and then the following named
were elected ofllcers of the verband- for
the ensuing year: Â¦''Â«'+ Z

first vice president. Simon Peisel. San Joae;
second vice president. Stephen Buhr, Los An
geles? third vice president, George Schilling,
San Francisco: recording secretary. Hy F.
Budde. San Francisco; corresponding secre
tary. Carl A. Schnabel, San Francisco; treas
urer, August Vetter. San Francisco; director.
Fr. Franz. Frultvale; director. L. Kaempfeu.
San Jose; director, Joseph Becker. San Fran
cisco.
It was resolved that next year's con
vention shall be hold at St. Anthony's
Church, San Francisco. "- | V
In the evening the delegates were ten
dered a delightful banquet in St. Boniface
Hall.

SPIRITLESS RACE.
NEW YORK, Aug. 31.â Again to-day
the beautiful cup defender Reliance failed
to register her third vistory over the
Lipton challenger because the wind died
to nothing and the time limit of five and
one-half hours expired before she could
reach the finish line.
.Her margin was approximately the
same as on Thursday last. She was less
than half a mile from the finish when the
gun sounded. The Shamrock III was
a faint blur on the horizon, fully two
and a. half miles astern. The Reliance's
failure to score the race was the' more
exasperating because, her ultimate tri
umph is now conceded, even by Sir Thom
as himself, to be simply a formality. The
superiority of the Herreshoff boat in any
kind of weather is acknowledged by the
yachting critics of both sides of the At
lantic, and to-day's fluke only prolongs
the agony of the, contest that has already
'been decided. ; i
The waning interest^ in the cup race was
strikingly illustrated by the size of the
observation fleet, if the ships which went
down to the ocean race course to-day
could be dignified by such a title. Out
side of the revenue cutters, which pa
trolled the \course, it consisted of half a
dozen tugs and a score of stearn, yachts.
The easterly wind and rain last night
had served to knock down the sea some
what, although it was rough -enough the
â¢first two hours of the race to make the
excursion Boats tumble about a good deal
and the yachts plunge and stagger on
their way to the outer mark. But as the
day wore on the wind hauled to the north
and fell to a mere zephyr, hardly blow-
Ing strong enougtt to round but the enor
mous canvases of the sloops and the sea
subsided to a smooth surface.
The race was spiritless from the begin
ning. It was laid fifteen miles to wind
ward up the Lon& Island shore and re
turn. Its savins feature was the start,
in which Captain Wringe, by a pretty
piece of maneuvering, neatly turned the
tables on tha Yankee skipper and cap
tured the windward, berth. Even so, the
yachts crossed the une abreast, like a
harnessed team of horses. '-'. ; i
! But within fifteen' minutes the defender
was showing her heels to the challenger
and .when the float at the outer mark
was reached she rounded it twenty min
utes and twenty-three seconds, ahead of
her rival. In the five times the boats have
met this was by ' far the worst beating
the Shamrock III has sustained in wind
ward work. The -only interest which re
mained after that was whether the Re
liance would be able to. reach the finish
Une in time to score the race.
The racers waited about' Sandy -Hook
lightship until 10:45, when the navigator
signaled that the starting line would be
shifted and the race postponed until later
in the day. After running five miles south
from the lightshjp the navigator signaled
a windward and leeward course, east
northeast, "from which direction a five
knot wind was blowing. The easterly
gale had left a long, heavy sea running,
and as the yachts jockeyed about the
starting line they lifted their long, bows
high out of the water on* top of the swell
and pounded the sea Into a troth.:- ,^.
WRINGE OUTGENERALS BARR.
For thp first time* in the series Cap
tain Wringe of Shamrock III cleverly
outgeneraled the redoubtable Captain
Barr In the'preliminary jockeying. Until
five minutes before the start Captain Barr
held the .windward berth. Then both ran
away from the line, the Shamrock lead
ing. Two minutes before the starting gun
the Shamrock headed ud into the wind,
tacked, forced the Reliance to do the
same and both ran for the line.-the Re
liance leading. They reached, it before
the starting sun, and the Reliance had
to run along the lee side of ft to await
the gun. The Shamrock reached the line
as the gun sounded and, not having to
sheer off, secured the weather position. JjJ
It was a trick which Captain Barr had
worked often, but never with better
finesse or more satisfactory results. Tne
official starting time 'of, both yachts was
11:43:26. iV^'r^lZ
The first tack .five minutes after cross
ing the line put the Shamrock III ahead,
but under the lee 'bow of the: Reliance.
Captain Wringe gave his boat a good rap
full and the way she began to walk away
from the defender made it look as though
she had found herself at last, but this
was for only a few minutes: When he
began to pinch her into wind it was ap
parent .that the Reliance was outpoint
ing the challenger and altogether making
better work of it.
They held on one tack east-southeast
for nearly an hour. During that time
the defender worked up to windward a
good quarter of a mile. When the Re
lianco tacked the Shamrock III refused
to follow suit. soBarr put his craft on
the port tack again. This placed the
Reliance a mile north of the Shamrock
III. Fifteen minutes later the wind
suddenly shifted to the' north/east and
placed the American boat that distance
dead to the windward, not half of which
she had actually earned. From that
time the Reliance gained steadily.
BELIANCE FAR AHEAD.
The yachts could head within two
points of their course. They \eld the
tack for twenty minutes and then made
short hitches, in which the Reliance
steadily got the better of it.
When off Long Beach, at 1:45, the de
fender was two miles ahead, haying
added the second mile by literally 'out
pointing the challenger and at the same
time maintaining equal speed. The
mark was then -in plain view and the
wind was softening. After a long board
off shore the Reliance made short
hitches, which brought her to the turn.
The challenger's cause was absolutely
hopeless when the derender rounded the
mark. The British boat was more than
two miles alee and coming slowly.
The time at the outer mark was: Re
liance, 3:00:37; Shamrock III., 3:21:00.
The Reliance led by twenty minutes
.and twenty-three seconds, barring time
allowance.
The Reliance \ at first reached home
ward under balloon jib; topsail and then
carried her spinnaker for a mile, only to
resort to the ballooner again. With
the wind, aft of the beam the main
sheet well In and the ballooner bellying
she made good; time and drew away
from the challenger until the wind soft
ened, when the speed of both slackened.
The Shamrock III caught a freshening
wind : first . and . cut down the Reliance's

At the Hofburg the King was received
by the Archdukes. Finance Minister Gol
choweki, the Austrian and Hungarian
Premiers and the court and state digni
taries. Thereafter the King retired to
his apartments, where he was officially
visited by the Emperor.
A state dinner In honor of King Ed
ward was given at the Hofburg to-night.
The company present Included the Em
peror, all the Archdukes and Arch
duchesses, the Ministers, diplomats and
the general staff of the army. Emperor
Francis Joseph toasted King Edward in
a speech, in the course of which he
hailed the latter's visit as a fresh pledge
for a continuance of the confidential re
lations which have Ions existed between
the two families and two countries and
which were all the firmer because there
was no political antagonism likely to
cast the shadow of a cloud upon them.
The King in response expressed his
thanks for the friendly sentiments ex
pressed toward him and th,e cordial re
ception he had met with, which he said
deeply touched him. He assured
the Emperor of the continuance of . the
feeling of friendship which he had In
herited from Queen Victoria and con
cluded by appointing Emperor Francis
Joseph a field marshal in the British
army.
This announcement created a deep 1m
pression and the. Emperor rose, bowed
and pressed King Edward's hand. Dur
ing the course of the dinner the mon
archs conversed with each other and
the guests in an animated manner.
Enormous crowds assembled outside the
palace, as they had done during the day.

ternational Congress of Actuaries met In

I am glad Indeed to greet you, not only ma

A letter from President Roosevelt, ex
pressing regret at his inability to be pres
ent, was read and after a' brief address
by President Peirson and a discussion of
papers on "The Improvement in Longev
ity," an adjournment was taken.
The subject of the long discussion, "Im
provement in Longevity," was taken up
in the afternoon. Charlton T. Lewis said
that there were three general reasons
for the Improvement in longevityâ the
better care that children received, tha
development In handling epidemics and
the vast Improvement in medical and
surgical science.
"We find," he said, "that the mortality
in particular diseases has been decreased
very greatly in the last forty years;
these influences are all alike In this re
spect, that all represent an Increase in
the vitality in the strong, but a diminu
tion of mortality among- the weak. The
number of weaklings is to-day therefore
greater, and the result Is a degradation
of the masses in the average."
F. L. Hoffman of Newark University
said in part:
"When we are told by a Secretary of
War that the mortality in Cuba is not
more than in the city of New York. It
does not require nvich knowledge to
smile and know that It is nonsense."
He declared that the statement that the
death rate of the city of Havana is no
greater than that of Washington City
is untrue. For sorr. Â¦> time .o come the
death rate of Havana will be from 50 to
100 per cent greater than any northern
city.

"Standing. room only" is still the word
at the entrance to Fischer's little comic
opera establishment. This is the last
week of "Quo'Vass Iss" and "The Big
Little Princess." Next week another
double bill of this sort of * side-splitters
takes the boards with, all the old favor
ites in the cast. The new musical director
of Fischer's Theater to succeed popular
Harry James is Robert Hood Bowers,
known in the musical .world as Robert
Hood. Mr. Bowers is but 26 years of age,
but has already achieved success as a
composer of music. He has composed the
music, for ".The Paraders," "Rubes and
Roses," "Tom-tom," "The Prince Con
sort," "Kitty Tralone," and ."The Mum
my and "the Maid," in addition to four
new productions which will be announced
1h the near future. Nearly all of the
â¢above named productions will be put on
at Fischer's Theater this fall and winter.
Besides his musical productions Mr. Bow
ers has composed more than 100'instru
mental pieces and fully as marry' vbcaf
numbers which have become very "popu
lar throughout the country. Mr. Bowefs
has a great experience for his age as an
orchestra conductor and has organized
three large orchestras in/ the' Middle
States which are still In existence.' "The
Tenderfoot," the musical comedy now
running in Chicago with', great ' success,
was scored for orchestra by Mr. Bowers.

FLORENCE ROBERTS reappeared
at the Alcazar "Theater last even
ing and secure^ a welcome that'
expressed itself in several cart-
loads of flowers* and a multitude
of curtain calls. The latter were_.so per
sistent that a speech was evidently the
only way to quiet the Roberts admirers.
It was a great opening night In point
of numbers. Every seat In the house was
taken and the standing room in the rear
was also occupied. When Florence
Roberts had all the flowers that had been
handed up to the stage properly arrayed
they made a bank that -extended nearly*
the whole width of the stage floor. She
came out, put a friendly hand upon the
biggest floral emblem of the lot, on which
was the Inscription "Welcome Home,"
and made a n.eat little talk.
"I- am glad that you are glad," she
said. "I thank you. If I.snould talk
half an hour it would be but variations
of that. These flowers will fade, their
perfume will fail, but the memory j of
this welcome will not fade; its perfume
will "be In my heart forever."
It was a Florence Roberts night in. all
particulars. The stock company rallied
around her as well as the writing of the
lines of the play would permit, but the
star part was entirely dominant from
beginning to end'. The stpry is one of
almost "unbroken woe. The discarded
wife, who creeps back to her home that
she may see Jier daughter, who supposes
her to be dead, this being on the daugh
ter's" wedding eve, -is the central motive.
This emotional' situation was handled,
with.- -much, cleverness. . Woe ranged
through the 'gamut and. tears were
commanded. at will. The work of the
leading rolejwa's clean cut and judicious.
It caught '.the audience strongly. Hope'
anfl despair- vibrated in the" lines Just
frequently enough to .â¢ keep the emo
tional interest on. edge, When .the end
came and .the. curtain fell, upon the play
just at' the .moment when all that "The
Unhappy Mrs. Hatch" could have hoped
for in the way of bliss was at hand and
she died, overcome with illness, the
house had lost none of Its crowding au
ditors. They stayed, one and all, until
there was no more chancejto be stirred.
Judging from all precedents and the
manner in which "The Unwelcome Mrs.
Hatch'" was received last evening it will
have a successful fun to' good nightly
business. It has all the peculiar twinges
that inhere in- a plot that provides for
the expulsion from her home of the un
fortunate discarded wife the second time;
that includes a romance in which the
suffering Mrs. Hatch refuses to accept an
eligible offer of marriage for fear that
she will drag down the man that she has
learned to love. Then there is a flicker
of gaycty, which soon goes out,-attend
ant upon the coming of the bridesmaids
to the wedding of the daughter. Also
there Is a scene in a park, where a lot
of boys and girls do stunts in a dramatic
way, that takes the house for their pure
ingenuousness. Marie Howe did" cleverly
with the part of the servant. Virginia
Brissac was . a nice little . stage bride.
Walter. Belasco .was in \ the park scene,
as a tramp, and is desperately made up
for the part.

He was explaining the workings of the

SPOKANE, Wash., Aug. 13.â Wlthi
hands tied together Mike Brogan, a
soldier at Fort Wright, and Lulu Hoff
man, 17 years old, sought to end their
love troubles this morning by jumping
from the foot bridge at Natatorium Park
into the Spokane River. Their Jump was
witnessed by a mail carrier for Fort
Wright, who a minute before had Jeered
at them as they stood on the bridge. The
letter carrier dashed madly into the- park,
where he summoned several laborers and
rushed back to drag the lovelorn pair
from tno water. Their services were
hardly necessary, because Brogan as soon
as his feet touched the water, repented,
wriggled himself partly free from tho,
bindings and began swimming toward
shore, dragging the woman with him.
More dead than alive the two were
dragged from the water. The young
woman was rolled on the bank for a few
minutes and taken to the engine room
at the baths, where she was given a
change of garments. Brogan was placed
under arrest and Is now In the guard
house.
According to the young woman, she has
been smitten with Brogan, but he did not
reciprocate her affections. The two met
this morning and Brogan unfolded- his
story. He told her that hla sweetheart
had Jilted him, that he could not Iovq
Miss Hoffman and urged that the two
end their troubles by jumping from the
bridge together. After an hour's urging
the girl consented to Brogan'a plan.

Overture," which Is rather difficult, it haa

rangement of the Rheindaughter trio

The novelties at this concert will be
Max Schilling's prelude to act II from
Zugwelde, and Edvard ' Grieg's symphonic
dance No. 4. These works are especially
pretty and artistic and will please every
one anxious to hear good music. It ap
pears that the attendance at thesa sym
phony concerts is growing rapidly and
judging from the advance sale-, the at
tendance will be even greater than at the
last concert. There cannot ba any doubt
of the fact that Scheel is the most pop
ular and most efficient symphony con
ductor we ever had In San Francisco and
the public Is manifesting this fact very
forcefully Just now.
Central.
The limit of the sensational and spec
tacular was reached at the Central The
ater last night in the production on a
stupendous scale of Augustin Daly's big
New York success, "The Great Ruby."
The scenes were as thrilling as the lurid
posters of the play could lead one to ex
pect and the guarantees of the advance
notices were more than fulfilled. The
performance Is elaborate and expensive.
There Is a whole jewelry store for the
setting of the first act and in the scenes
that follow the players ride about In
genuine automobiles, on bicycles antt in
tallyhos and victorias. Theii are four
in-hands, single teams and saddle horses
and, as if all the splendid turnouts men
tioned were not enough for the
wild, mad chase, "The Great Ruby" is
finally carried up in a balloon, and this
balloon ascension is the grand clizriax of
the drama. In fact this balloon scene
is seldom surpassed on any stage for
realistic effects. The two arch conspir
ators. Prince Kasslm (Herschel Mayall)
and Morris Longman (Henry Schumer).
are driven to desperation. They hold
the priceless ruby, and to cheat the pur
suing detectives they cut loose in a bal
loon .which an aeronaut has left ready
for flight. S The city sinks below them
and they quarrel as they mount" upi into
the clouds and Prince Kasslm hurls his
antagonist down to destruction. This
scene made the'lmmense audience Wildly
enthusiastic and a half dozen curtain
calls followed. The cast is- a very long
one. There are forty speaking parts and
over, 100 people in the production. The
costumes of the ladies axe elegant. Miss
Eugenie. Thais Lawton was charming as
Lady ; Gamett. ambitious to shine In the
smart set. and Miss Genevieve Kane was
very effective as the adventuress. "The
Great Ruby" is the kind of a perform
ance that the .Central folk fairly revel
In. -It fills the bill in every respect and
will pack the house all the week.
Grand Opera-House.
A. very enjoyable evening can be spent
at the Grand Opera-house any night this
week. Pollard's Lilliputian Opera Com
pany. i3 there with "The Belle of New
York." Every one of the numerous little
Pollards is a clever actor jand the other
little ones in the cast are equally as good.
California.
The clever company at the California
Theater scored another pronounced suc
cess last night in Henry Arthur Jones'
play, "Mrs. Dane's Defense." 4 The the
ater was crowded and the principals re
ceived several curtain calls.

Boy's Hip. Broken by'Streetcari.
â¢Chester Johnson," -6' yeJvrs -old, -. who
lives 'with- his. parents. at 703 Tennessee
street, -was : severely injured . yesterday
afternoon on Kentucky street, near Nine
teenth, .by -an Eighth street trolley car.
Johnson, with other boys .sibout his own
age. was crossing" the street .when" a
south-bound car, in charge of Motorman
John Leach, knocked him .down; fracfu'r
ing the bone, of his right hip. \The little
fellow was removed to tho home of his
parents, where he was attended by Dr.
Smith. Sergeant Lewis of the Potre.ro
police station arrested the motorman
and charged him with battery.

While railroading is his business, Mr.
Kendrick takes a lively Interest in na
tional politics, and he is a firm believer
In the certainty of the re-election of
Roosevelt.
Vice President Kendrick is of the opin
ion that the recent flurry of stocks in
.Wall street will In the end have a bene
ficial influence on railroads all over the
country. In that It will tend to discourage
the inflation of stocks. He thinks that
the break" was 'effectedf for no other pur
pose than to bring the stocks down to a
figure near their real value, and he also
believes that the financiers will endeavor
to keep them thert. *. . -
As to the new equipment for the West
ern system of the Santa Fe, he said that
the company has already placed orders
I for forty-five mountain engines for the
Arizona division and for 500 new refrig
erating cars for the Southern California
lines. The latter, he said, were contract
ed to be delivered to the company dur
ing the early part of January next.
As yet the company has not received a
complete estimate of its losses by the re
cent floods In the Middle West, and Vice
President Kendrick does not think that
one will ever be made. Figures at hand,
however, show that the Santa Fe lO3t
about 2^00 feet of track before the waters
subsided.
."Jo'hn W. kendrick. third vice president,
and A. "W*. "Wells, general manager of .the
Santa Fe road, arrived from Los Angeles
oh "yesterday afternoon's train and regis
tered- at the Palace Hotel. They .have
come north to look over the various im
provements being made by their company
across the bay,' and while in the north
the first named expects to carefully re
view the plans. of Chfef- Engineer Storey
for. the construction of the new road be
tween here and Eureka. ... â¢ "'â¢
'. Iii referring, to the future plana of tha
road Mr. Kendrick said last evening that
the company was disposed to go a bit
slow, especially in the matter of expend
Ing money for expansion, and he thought
that this was. the disposition of other
large railroad concerns in the country.
As to the extension to Eureka he said:
â¢ "While the plans will certainly be car
ried-out,- we do 'not purpose to rush thÂ«
wock'of active construction. Preliminary
.surveys are being made, and when we
get ready we will proceed building into
one of the richest timber sections of the
State."
"i'lip first Intimation I received that I had
been^ guilty of any attempt to plead ther pauper
act, *a3 they term It. wa3 to-day. My attor
ney, Frank Murphy, told me wh;n the Insol
vency proceedings were on that there was no
need of my paying- the court fee. and he- set
.me to sisn a paper, the contents of which I
never read. If I had realized what Its purport
was I would have quickly paid the fee.
My financial troubles were due to a business
undertaking in the Philippines In which I was
associated with another. It proved a failure
and later I signed notes to cover my portion
of the indebtedness. I afterward found that
I could not meet these not*s and. although I
have many rich friends In this city from whom
I could have borrowed the necessary money,
I refused to do so and went through Insol
vency. If Cleric Morse is determined to get
that ?". I suppose that I will have to dig It
up for him.

The plumbers of Portland are out on strike. While
this may result in some annoyances and exasperating
inconveniences to the people of the city, owners of
homes will be relieved from any immediate necessity

Death, of Dr. A. Atherton.
A message was received in this city
from New York yesterday announcing
the death there of Dr. Alexander Ather
' ton, son of the fate J. B. Atherton, a
wealthy sugar planter of Honolulu. The
senior Atherton died In Honolulu several
weeks ago of consumption, and It was the
same disease that carried -off his son. The
latter had only recently graduated as a
physician, and had taken up the study of
tuberculosis. Young Atherton is survived
by a widow, who was a bride of only a
few days.

PASSED OFFICER-Subscriber. City. A
passed officer In a fraternal organization
is one who has completed a full term and
is retired as a passed (sometimes written
past) master in Freemasonry. One who
has filled the chair of master of a lodge,
when his term has been completed Is the
youngest passed master, consequently is
the junior passed master. If in
a fraternal body, as is the case
in some orders," the outgoing pre
siding officer fills the office, gener
ally termed passed master, president, etc.,
such individual is the junior passed. The
one who retires from that chair and. gives
way to the junior is, on retiring, known
in the order as a passed officer and not as
junior, as there cannot be two juniors of
the same rank, in a fraternal body.

. PPQ>1IN^NT. OF: THE-~NATJVE . glON'S OF THE GOL.DEX- WEST; "VVHO. ARE TAKING" AX- ACTIVE
:'.'Â¦. PART. I N v MA-KIN''G-.'ANiJ:C6mPL : ETJNG ELABORATE WRRAN.GEMENTS . FOR' -T-HE FITTING.. OBSERV- .
. â¢.'â¢ ANCe' PFTHE^TATE'SBIRTHDAY/'AT-SANJOSE, ON SEPTEMBER 9. . . " .' r .'. *' ." T '. "
Pauncefote in -iooo. â¢ ; ' .''â¢'..-. Â¦ ';" .'.â¢.'
About the utmost that can be expected : of the com
mission is that .some agreement will be reached ..-as
to the essential facts of the case and a foundation ; be ;
thus laid for a.'future settlement of. the issue.'. Even
should there be disagreement upon . some .essential
points the statement of the disagreement will' have,
the effect of clearly defining. the points of antagonism
and to that extent will -aid. in preparing for/dealing'
with them justly and satisfactorily at some later .time. â¢
Such being the" case popular .interest jn the" or-bceed-:
ings of the commission will be-directed mainly to" the "
form in which' the arguments of the contending par-
ties arc presented and will be almost as : rmich" ac"a-..
demic as political. 'Â¦ . " . ". '. .* ' ' .'Â¦'Â¦Â¦Â¦'Â¦Â¦
The question has been referred to a joint cotnmis- â¢
sion rather than to a board of arbitration"because.the >
United States' Senate and indeed a majority -of tb,e*
American people are unwilling to submit to the arbi
tration of any foreign power the. question of the
rightfulness of cur sovereignty over the disputed ter
ritory. â¢ It is .difficult indeed for us to understand
that Canada has any foundation whatever for her de
mand that we concede' to her territory, that has long
been in our possession, andwas much longer in Rus
sian possession, undisputed by any one.
It is well known that the claim of the Canadians'
arose out of a desire on their part to get a port for
the Klondike territory. Had there been no gold dis
coveries in that part of the world there would never
have been any contention against our occupation and
sovereignty over the strip of land that shuts the
Canadians out from access to the sea. It is a familiar
truth that the wish is father to the thought. Canada
strongly wishes a port of her own opposite the Klon
dike, and out of that wish has arisen first the thought
that she has a right to such a port and later the claim "
that we are wrongfully depriving her of her territory.
The American case, based upon the undisputed oc
cupation of the territory by Russia, from whom we
obtained our title, is confirmed by the language of
the treaty between Russia and Great Britain on the
subject and by official statements in written docu
ments, of British and of Russian diplomats. The
treaty in defining the line along the disputed strip
says it shall be "formed by a line parallel to the sin
uosities of the coast and which shall never exceed the
distance of ten marine leagues therefrom." The lan
guage itself is clear, but it is made even clearer by
statements of the negotiators as to the intent of the
reservation to Russia of the strip along the coast.
M. de Polctica in a report to Count Nesselrode said:
"In fixing the longitude Count de Lambert had
mainly in view the establishment of a barritr at
which would be estopped once and for all, to the
north as well as to the west of the coast allotted to
our American company, the encroachments .of the
Hudson Bay Company."
It is thus clear that the prime object of the treaty
on the part of the Russians was to shut out the Hud
son Bay Company from the sea, and the limits were
fixed expressly for that purpose. The United States
has succeeded to the rights of Russia and Canada
has succeeded to the rights of the Hudson Bay Com
pany. It will therefore require the exercise of a
good deal of ingenuity on the part of the British
commissioners to make even a good pretense of a
showing to justify the Canadian claims.

are. essentially public.
The various municipal commissions', .which hold
office by 'authority oÂ£ the Mayor, har.dly' "have- a'
session -these days without the -enlivening,- if "hot
edifying",. spectacle .of. a rc"w. "-This is as .i.t should*
be.. Tke more -'the commissioners qtiarrel the "more
tax -payers .and. the public -.will learn of- affajrs. which
Intelligent citizenship will continue, to insi'st; that
this city and Oakland, embarking 4ipon municipal
ownership, shall be in line with the* conclusion of
experience and rest each bond issue for that purpo'se
upon the plant which it acquires or creates. The
wise proponents of public ownership must themselves
see that such policy increases the reasons for econ
omy in administration and enhances the probability
of success.
Already in Oakland the proposition to bond the
.whole city for a water plant is being closely followed
by one to bond it for a telephone system, and the
propaganda for the latter is already well under way.
Unless a right start is made both cities will find their
general fund burdened beyond. the possibility of the
use of any of it for the essential purposes of govern
ment and their constitutional credit limit exhausted,
reaving them powerless to effect other betterments
recognized by all as the proper function of govern
ment.
THE taxpayers are called upon to vote an issue
of bonds for acquisition to city ownership and
operation of the Geary-street Railroad.*"
The ballot sets forth the purpose of the" issue,. so
that every voter, may read and know what it is, as fol
lows: "Proposition to acquire by original construc
tion and completion by the city and county of San
Francisco, a public utility, to wit, a. street railro'ad,
and to incur a bonded debt of the city and county to
the amount of seven hundred and ten thousand* ($7.10,
000) dollars, with interest at three ami a half per cent
per annum/ for such acquisition o.f. same; the route of
such railroad to be. as follows: 'Commencing from
Fulton street on Tenth avenue,, along Tenth .avenue,

The suit was a novel one in that the
plaintiff sought to show that Living
ston, though ostensibly a money lender,
was in reality a pawnbroker who charged'
a hither rate of interest than the law
allowed for his advances. Beeiuse of this
fact, the plaintiff claimed, he should be
compelled to give up the jewels he rÂ«
ceived from Schumacher and take his
chances with the rest of the creditors.
He failed to establish his claim, how
ever, even though Schumacher admitted
on the stand that he had .acted as an
agent for Livingston, and a verdict in
his favor wan the result.

WASHINGTON, Aug. 31,â Secretary
Wilson has asked the Department- " of
Commerce r and. Labor to ascertain
through the consular service suitable
markets for macaroni wheat, a hard
grain common in European countries, and
crops of which are increasing rapidly in
the United States.
' Secretary Wilson says the macaroni
wheat yield In this country -this year, ac
cording to the* most commonly accepted
estimate, is 10,000,000 bushels, against
2,000,000 bushels a year ago, and he pre

MARKETS ARE NEEDED
' " . FOR MACARONI WHEAT
ST. PAUL.. Minn, Aug. 31.â Sixteen thou
sand saw Dan Patch unsuccessfully at
tempt to lower his world's record at. the
State Fair grounds ? to-day. Despite the
fact that' the great pacer- had gone
against time at Readville, Mass., and had
been almost continuously on the cars
until to-day, he, went a mile in two min
utes. The weather was ideal, being
warm, with scarcely a breeze stirring
and the track was In first-class condlUon.
Accompanied by his -running mate. Old
Mike, the stallion was off at the second
scoring, covering the ground in long,
easy, strides. The first quarter was gone
In 30 seconds, the next in 23%, the half
being made in 59%. Around the far turn
the runner slackened his speed a trifle
and Patch did the third quarter in SO?;.
Coming into the stretch. Patch showed a
magnificent burst of speed, and with Mc-
Hcnry fairly lifting him along, the last
quarter was done In 29^ and the mile in
two minutes.
Dan Patch Paces Mile in Two Min
utes at St.' -Paul.

JUDGE DE HAVEN PASSES. / /â¢
. ..SENTENCE ON PBISONEBS
Attorney Shortridge. Makes' Motion
; for a New Trial on Behalf of
,'-. W. H. Dillard.
A batch Qf. prisoners appeared before
United- States â¢; District Judge de
Haven- yesterday, to" receive sen
tence- ' Tim Summerly, John Phelan,
S. : A. .. Peterson, .John Wilson and
Tilly' Jorgeson, sailors, who had pleaded
guilty to smuggling 100 pounds of white
lead; from ".the British" ship D.umfrieshire,
â¢were each sentenced : to : pay'a fine of $50
or, in. default, to "be" imprisoned in the
County Jail until .the 'fine should be paid.
Theodore *t\V Johnson 'and- Emil Daniel
son,-for assaulting United States Gauge'r
Frank. H. Dfiscoll while in the perform
ance of his duty,- were- sentenced. to.thre.e
months' i-m'prisbnment-ln ihe .County Jail.-
. In the matter.-of the conviction of'Wil-.
Ham H. Dillard for .issuing false and
-fraudulent certificates of ' registr'ation,
Samuel M. Shortridge announced that he
desired to make a motion for a new trial*.
Next Saturday was se't as the day. on
which the argument on the' motion will
be held. . : ; /^. '* . .
Painter Falls to His Death.
Emedio Higuera, a painter, who resided
in Oakla.nd, fell from a -scaffolding while
painting a house at 1448 Willard street
yesterday. He was removed to the Park
Emergency Hospital, where it was found
that he had suffered a fracture of the
spinal column. He was later removed to
the Central Emergency Hospital, where
be died last night.

WASHINGTON, -Aqg. 31.â As a result
of an Investigation* of the â¢question. 'Act
ing Secretary Darling of the Navy De
â¢partrnent has concluded that there la no
occasion for any action by the Navy De
partment at this time on the proposition
that all employes of the Navy Depart
ment and navy yards be required to take
the oath of allegiance to the United
States. He found on Inquiry that only
citizens of the United States and only
such non-citizens as have been honor
ably discharged from the army, navy or
marine corps are eligible for employment
in the na-val eataJMtshment.
These requirements, he considered, give
sufficient -guarantee of loyajty to the
United , States of the men In^the employ
of the civil naval establishment.

antee" Loyalty to Country.
